   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Ecosystem
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      This article is about natural ecosystems.  For the term used in man-made systems, see
      <a href="/wiki/Digital_ecosystem" title="Digital ecosystem">
       Digital ecosystem
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/220px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/330px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/440px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">
         Coral reefs
        </a>
        are a highly
        <a href="/wiki/Productivity_(ecology)" title="Productivity (ecology)">
         productive
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">
         marine ecosystem
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-1">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          1
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="691" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif/220px-River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif/330px-River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif/440px-River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:River_gambia_Niokolokoba_National_Park.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">
         Rainforest
        </a>
        ecosystems are rich in
        <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
         biodiversity
        </a>
        . This is the
        <a href="/wiki/Gambia_River" title="Gambia River">
         Gambia River
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">
         Senegal
        </a>
        's
        <a href="/wiki/Niokolo-Koba_National_Park" title="Niokolo-Koba National Park">
         Niokolo-Koba National Park
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An
      <b>
       ecosystem
      </b>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Community_(ecology)" title="Community (ecology)">
       community
      </a>
      of living organisms in conjunction with the
      <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">
       nonliving components
      </a>
      of their environment (things like air, water and mineral soil), interacting as a system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These biotic and
      <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">
       abiotic components
      </a>
      are regarded as linked together through nutrient cycles and energy flows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum1971_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Odum1971-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As ecosystems are defined by the network of interactions among organisms, and between organisms and their environment,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulze400_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schulze400-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      they can be of any size but usually encompass specific, limited spaces
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (although some scientists say that the entire planet is an ecosystem).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planet_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-planet-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Energy, water, nitrogen and soil minerals are other essential abiotic components of an ecosystem. The energy that flows through ecosystems is obtained primarily from the sun. It generally enters the system through
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      , a process that also captures
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      from the atmosphere. By feeding on plants and on one another,
      <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">
       animals
      </a>
      play an important role in the movement of matter and energy through the system. They also influence the quantity of plant and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">
       microbial
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">
       biomass
      </a>
      present. By breaking down dead organic matter,
      <a href="/wiki/Decomposer" title="Decomposer">
       decomposers
      </a>
      release carbon back to the atmosphere and facilitate
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycling" title="Nutrient cycling">
       nutrient cycling
      </a>
      by converting nutrients stored in dead biomass back to a form that can be readily used by plants and other microbes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin10_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin10-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Ecosystems are controlled both by external and internal factors. External factors such as
      <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">
       climate
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Parent_material" title="Parent material">
       parent material
      </a>
      which forms the soil and
      <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">
       topography
      </a>
      , control the overall structure of an ecosystem and the way things work within it, but are not themselves influenced by the ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other external factors include time and potential
      <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">
       biota
      </a>
      . Ecosystems are dynamic entitiesinvariably, they are subject to periodic disturbances and are in the process of recovering from some past disturbance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ecosystems in similar environments that are located in different parts of the world can have very different characteristics simply because they contain different species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
       introduction of non-native species
      </a>
      can cause substantial shifts in ecosystem function. Internal factors not only control ecosystem processes but are also controlled by them and are often subject to
      <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">
       feedback loops
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the
      <a href="/wiki/Resource_(biology)" title="Resource (biology)">
       resource
      </a>
      inputs are generally controlled by external processes like climate and parent material, the availability of these resources within the ecosystem is controlled by internal factors like decomposition, root competition or shading.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other internal factors include disturbance, succession and the types of species present. Although humans exist and operate within ecosystems, their cumulative effects are large enough to influence external factors like climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       Biodiversity
      </a>
      affects ecosystem function, as do the processes of
      <a href="/wiki/Disturbance_(ecology)" title="Disturbance (ecology)">
       disturbance
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">
       succession
      </a>
      . Ecosystems provide a variety of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">
       goods and services
      </a>
      upon which people depend; the principles of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_management" title="Ecosystem management">
       ecosystem management
      </a>
      suggest that rather than managing individual species,
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">
       natural resources
      </a>
      should be managed at the level of the ecosystem itself. Classifying ecosystems into ecologically homogeneous units is an important step towards effective ecosystem management, but there is no single, agreed-upon way to do this.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_development">
       History and development
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and development">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The term "ecosystem" was first used in a publication by British ecologist
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Tansley" title="Arthur Tansley">
       Arthur Tansley
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        fn 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis1997_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Willis1997-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tansley devised the concept to draw attention to the importance of transfers of materials between organisms and their environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin7_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He later refined the term, describing it as "The whole system, ... including not only the organism-complex, but also the whole complex of physical factors forming what we call the environment".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansley1935_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tansley1935-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tansley regarded ecosystems not simply as natural units, but as mental isolates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansley1935_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tansley1935-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tansley later
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansley1939_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tansley1939-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      defined the spatial extent of ecosystems using the term
      <a href="/wiki/Ecotope" title="Ecotope">
       ecotope
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/G._Evelyn_Hutchinson" title="G. Evelyn Hutchinson">
       G. Evelyn Hutchinson
      </a>
      , a pioneering
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limnologist" title="Limnologist">
       limnologist
      </a>
      who was a contemporary of Tansley's, combined
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sutherland_Elton" title="Charles Sutherland Elton">
       Charles Elton
      </a>
      's ideas about
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/trophic" title="wikt:Special:Search/trophic">
       trophic
      </a>
      ecology with those of Russian geochemist
      <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Vernadsky" title="Vladimir Vernadsky">
       Vladimir Vernadsky
      </a>
      to suggest that mineral nutrient availability in a lake limited algal
      <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">
       production
      </a>
      which would, in turn, limit the abundance of animals that feed on algae.
      <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Lindeman" title="Raymond Lindeman">
       Raymond Lindeman
      </a>
      took these ideas one step further to suggest that the flow of energy through a lake was the primary driver of the ecosystem. Hutchinson's students, brothers
      <a href="/wiki/Howard_T._Odum" title="Howard T. Odum">
       Howard T. Odum
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_P._Odum" title="Eugene P. Odum">
       Eugene P. Odum
      </a>
      , further developed a "systems approach" to the study of ecosystems, allowing them to study the flow of energy and material through ecological systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin7_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_processes">
       Ecosystem processes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ecosystem processes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       Energy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      enter ecosystems through photosynthesis, are incorporated into living tissue, transferred to other organisms that feed on the living and dead plant matter, and eventually released through respiration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin123_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin123-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most mineral nutrients, on the other hand, are recycled within ecosystems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Ecosystems are controlled both by external and internal factors. External factors, also called state factors, control the overall structure of an ecosystem and the way things work within it, but are not themselves influenced by the ecosystem. The most important of these is
      <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">
       climate
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Climate determines the
      <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">
       biome
      </a>
      in which the ecosystem is embedded. Rainfall patterns and temperature seasonality determine the amount of water available to the ecosystem and the supply of energy available (by influencing photosynthesis).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Parent_material" title="Parent material">
       Parent material
      </a>
      , the underlying geological material that gives rise to soils, determines the nature of the soils present, and influences the supply of mineral nutrients.
      <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">
       Topography
      </a>
      also controls ecosystem processes by affecting things like
      <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">
       microclimate
      </a>
      , soil development and the movement of water through a system. This may be the difference between the ecosystem present in
      <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">
       wetland
      </a>
      situated in a small depression on the landscape, and one present on an adjacent steep hillside.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other external factors that play an important role in ecosystem functioning include time and potential
      <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">
       biota
      </a>
      . Ecosystems are dynamic entitiesinvariably, they are subject to periodic disturbances and are in the process of recovering from some past disturbance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Time plays a role in the development of soil from bare rock and the
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">
       recovery of a community from disturbance
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similarly, the set of organisms that can potentially be present in an area can also have a major impact on ecosystems. Ecosystems in similar environments that are located in different parts of the world can end up doing things very differently simply because they have different pools of species present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
       introduction of non-native species
      </a>
      can cause substantial shifts in ecosystem function.
     </p>
     <p>
      Unlike external factors, internal factors in ecosystems not only control ecosystem processes, but are also controlled by them. Consequently, they are often subject to
      <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">
       feedback loops
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the
      <a href="/wiki/Resource_(biology)" title="Resource (biology)">
       resource
      </a>
      inputs are generally controlled by external processes like climate and parent material, the availability of these resources within the ecosystem is controlled by internal factors like decomposition, root competition or shading.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other factors like disturbance, succession or the types of species present are also internal factors. Human activities are important in almost all ecosystems. Although humans exist and operate within ecosystems, their cumulative effects are large enough to influence external factors like climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin11_8-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin11-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_production">
       Primary production
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Primary production">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="1280" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/400px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/600px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/800px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Global oceanic and terrestrial
        <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">
         phototroph
        </a>
        abundance, from September 1997 to August 2000. As an estimate of
        <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">
         autotroph
        </a>
        biomass, it is only a rough indicator of primary production potential, and not an actual estimate of it. Provided by the
        <a href="/wiki/SeaWiFS" title="SeaWiFS">
         SeaWiFS
        </a>
        Project,
        <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
         NASA
        </a>
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
         Goddard Space Flight Center
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ORBIMAGE" title="ORBIMAGE">
         ORBIMAGE
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">
       Primary production
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Primary production is the production of
      <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">
       organic matter
      </a>
      from inorganic carbon sources. Overwhelmingly, this occurs through photosynthesis. The energy incorporated through this process supports life on earth, while the carbon makes up much of the organic matter in living and dead biomass,
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_carbon" title="Soil carbon">
       soil carbon
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuels
      </a>
      . It also drives the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon cycle
      </a>
      , which influences global
      <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">
       climate
      </a>
      via the
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
       greenhouse effect
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Through the process of photosynthesis, plants capture energy from light and use it to combine
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      and water to produce
      <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">
       carbohydrates
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
       oxygen
      </a>
      . The photosynthesis carried out by all the plants in an ecosystem is called the gross primary production (GPP).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin97_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin97-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      About 4860% of the GPP is consumed in plant respiration. The remainder, that portion of GPP that is not used up by respiration, is known as the net primary production (NPP).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin123_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin123-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Total photosynthesis is limited by a range of environmental factors. These include the amount of light available, the amount of
      <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">
       leaf
      </a>
      area a plant has to capture light (shading by other plants is a major limitation of photosynthesis), rate at which carbon dioxide can be supplied to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">
       chloroplasts
      </a>
      to support photosynthesis, the availability of water, and the availability of suitable temperatures for carrying out
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin97_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin97-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_flow">
       Energy flow
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Energy flow">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright" style="background:#f9f9f9; border:1px solid #ccc; margin:0.5em;">
      <p>
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EnergyFlowFrog.jpg">
        <img alt="EnergyFlowFrog.jpg" data-file-height="1111" data-file-width="1576" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/EnergyFlowFrog.jpg/200px-EnergyFlowFrog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/EnergyFlowFrog.jpg/300px-EnergyFlowFrog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/EnergyFlowFrog.jpg/400px-EnergyFlowFrog.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg">
        <img alt="EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="633" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg/200px-EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg/300px-EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg/400px-EnergyFlowTransformity.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
      </p>
      <div style="border: none; width:375px;">
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <b>
         Left:
        </b>
        Energy flow diagram of a frog. The frog represents a node in an extended food web. The energy ingested is utilized for metabolic processes and transformed into biomass. The energy flow continues on its path if the frog is ingested by predators, parasites, or as a decaying
        <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">
         carcass
        </a>
        in soil. This energy flow diagram illustrates how energy is lost as it fuels the metabolic process that transforms the energy and nutrients into biomass.
        <br/>
        <b>
         Right:
        </b>
        An expanded three link energy food chain (1. plants, 2. herbivores, 3. carnivores) illustrating the relationship between food flow diagrams and energy transformity. The transformity of energy becomes degraded, dispersed, and diminished from higher quality to lesser quantity as the energy within a food chain flows from one trophic species into another. Abbreviations: I=input, A=assimilation, R=respiration, NU=not utilized, P=production, B=biomass.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum88_18-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Odum88-18">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          17
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_flow_(ecology)" title="Energy flow (ecology)">
       Energy flow (ecology)
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">
       Food web
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">
       Trophic level
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The carbon and energy incorporated into plant tissues (net primary production) is either consumed by animals while the plant is alive, or it remains uneaten when the plant tissue dies and becomes
      <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">
       detritus
      </a>
      . In
      <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecosystem" title="Terrestrial ecosystem">
       terrestrial ecosystems
      </a>
      , roughly 90% of the NPP ends up being broken down by
      <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">
       decomposers
      </a>
      . The remainder is either consumed by animals while still alive and enters the plant-based trophic system, or it is consumed after it has died, and enters the detritus-based trophic system. In
      <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">
       aquatic systems
      </a>
      , the proportion of plant biomass that gets consumed by
      <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">
       herbivores
      </a>
      is much higher.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin244_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin244-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In trophic systems photosynthetic organisms are the primary producers. The organisms that consume their tissues are called primary consumers or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_production" title="Secondary production">
       secondary producers
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivores" title="Herbivores">
       herbivores
      </a>
      . Organisms which feed on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">
       microbes
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">
       fungi
      </a>
      ) are termed
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Microbivore&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Microbivore (page does not exist)">
       microbivores
      </a>
      . Animals that feed on primary consumers
      <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">
       carnivores
      </a>
      are secondary consumers. Each of these constitutes a
      <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">
       trophic level
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin244_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin244-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sequence of consumptionfrom plant to herbivore, to carnivoreforms a
      <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">
       food chain
      </a>
      . Real systems are much more complex than thisorganisms will generally feed on more than one form of food, and may feed at more than one trophic level. Carnivores may capture some prey which are part of a plant-based trophic system and others that are part of a detritus-based trophic system (a bird that feeds both on herbivorous grasshoppers and earthworms, which consume detritus). Real systems, with all these complexities, form
      <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">
       food webs
      </a>
      rather than food chains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin244_19-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin244-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Decomposition">
       Decomposition
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Decomposition">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">
       Decomposition
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The carbon and nutrients in
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">
       dead organic matter
      </a>
      are broken down by a group of processes known as decomposition. This releases nutrients that can then be re-used for plant and microbial production, and returns carbon dioxide to the atmosphere (or water) where it can be used for photosynthesis. In the absence of decomposition, dead organic matter would accumulate in an ecosystem and nutrients and atmospheric carbon dioxide would be depleted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Approximately 90% of terrestrial NPP goes directly from plant to decomposer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin244_19-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin244-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Decomposition processes can be separated into three categories
      <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(agriculture)" title="Leaching (agriculture)">
       leaching
      </a>
      , fragmentation and chemical alteration of dead material. As water moves through dead organic matter, it dissolves and carries with it the water-soluble components. These are then taken up by organisms in the soil, react with mineral soil, or are transported beyond the confines of the ecosystem (and are considered "lost" to it).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Newly shed leaves and newly dead animals have high concentrations of water-soluble components, and include
      <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">
       sugars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">
       amino acids
      </a>
      and mineral nutrients. Leaching is more important in wet environments, and much less important in dry ones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fragmentation processes break organic material into smaller pieces, exposing new surfaces for colonization by microbes. Freshly shed
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaf_litter" title="Leaf litter">
       leaf litter
      </a>
      may be inaccessible due to an outer layer of
      <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">
       cuticle
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Bark" title="Bark">
       bark
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Protoplasm" title="Protoplasm">
       cell contents
      </a>
      are protected by a
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">
       cell wall
      </a>
      . Newly dead animals may be covered by an
      <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">
       exoskeleton
      </a>
      . Fragmentation processes, which break through these protective layers, accelerate the rate of microbial decomposition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Animals fragment detritus as they hunt for food, as does passage through the gut.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeze-thaw_cycle" title="Freeze-thaw cycle">
       Freeze-thaw cycles
      </a>
      and cycles of wetting and drying also fragment dead material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The chemical alteration of dead organic matter is primarily achieved through bacterial and fungal action. Fungal
      <a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">
       hyphae
      </a>
      produce enzymes which can break through the tough outer structures surrounding dead plant material. They also produce enzymes which break down
      <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">
       lignin
      </a>
      , which allows to them access to both cell contents and to the nitrogen in the lignin. Fungi can transfer carbon and nitrogen through their hyphal networks and thus, unlike bacteria, are not dependent solely on locally available resources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin151_20-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin151-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Decomposition rates vary among ecosystems. The rate of decomposition is governed by three sets of factorsthe physical environment (temperature, moisture and soil properties), the quantity and quality of the dead material available to decomposers, and the nature of the microbial community itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Temperature controls the rate of microbial respiration; the higher the temperature, the faster microbial decomposition occurs. It also affects soil moisture, which slows microbial growth and reduces leaching. Freeze-thaw cycles also affect decompositionfreezing temperatures kill soil microorganisms, which allows leaching to play a more important role in moving nutrients around. This can be especially important as the soil thaws in the Spring, creating a pulse of nutrients which become available.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Decomposition rates are low under very wet or very dry conditions. Decomposition rates are highest in wet, moist conditions with adequate levels of oxygen. Wet soils tend to become deficient in oxygen (this is especially true in
      <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">
       wetlands
      </a>
      ), which slows microbial growth. In dry soils, decomposition slows as well, but bacteria continue to grow (albeit at a slower rate) even after soils become too dry to support plant growth. When the rains return and soils become wet, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osmotic_gradient" title="Osmotic gradient">
       osmotic gradient
      </a>
      between the bacterial cells and the soil water causes the cells to gain water quickly. Under these conditions, many bacterial cells burst, releasing a pulse of nutrients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Decomposition rates also tend to be slower in acidic soils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soils which are rich in
      <a href="/wiki/Clay_minerals" title="Clay minerals">
       clay minerals
      </a>
      tend to have lower decomposition rates, and thus, higher levels of organic matter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The smaller particles of clay result in a larger surface area that can hold water. The higher the water content of a soil, the lower the oxygen content
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin61_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin61-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and consequently, the lower the rate of decomposition. Clay minerals also bind particles of organic material to their surface, making them less accessibly to microbes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soil disturbance like
      <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">
       tilling
      </a>
      increase decomposition by increasing the amount of oxygen in the soil and by exposing new organic matter to soil microbes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The quality and quantity of the material available to decomposers is another major factor that influences the rate of decomposition. Substances like sugars and amino acids decompose readily and are considered "labile".
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">
       Cellulose
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">
       hemicellulose
      </a>
      , which are broken down more slowly, are "moderately labile". Compounds which are more resistant to decay, like lignin or
      <a href="/wiki/Cutin" title="Cutin">
       cutin
      </a>
      , are considered "recalcitrant".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Litter with a higher proportion of labile compounds decomposes much more rapidly than does litter with a higher proportion of recalcitrant material. Consequently, dead animals decompose more rapidly than dead leaves, which themselves decompose more rapidly than fallen branches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As organic material in the soil ages, its quality decreases. The more labile compounds decompose quickly, leaving an increasing proportion of recalcitrant material. Microbial cell walls also contain recalcitrant materials like
      <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">
       chitin
      </a>
      , and these also accumulate as the microbes die, further reducing the quality of older
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">
       soil organic matter
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin159_21-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin159-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrient_cycling">
       Nutrient cycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nutrient cycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycle" title="Nutrient cycle">
       Nutrient cycle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">
       Biogeochemical cycle
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">
       Nitrogen cycle
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="475" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg/400px-Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nitrogen_Cycle.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Biological nitrogen cycling
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ecosystems continually exchange energy and carbon with the wider
      <a href="/wiki/Environment_(systems)" title="Environment (systems)">
       environment
      </a>
      ; mineral nutrients, on the other hand, are mostly cycled back and forth between plants, animals, microbes and the soil. Most nitrogen enters ecosystems through biological
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">
       nitrogen fixation
      </a>
      , is deposited through precipitation, dust, gases or is applied as
      <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">
       fertilizer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since most terrestrial ecosystems are nitrogen-limited, nitrogen cycling is an important control on ecosystem production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Until modern times, nitrogen fixation was the major source of nitrogen for ecosystems. Nitrogen fixing bacteria either live
      <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">
       symbiotically
      </a>
      with plants, or live freely in the soil. The energetic cost is high for plants which support nitrogen-fixing symbiontsas much as 25% of GPP when measured in controlled conditions. Many members of the
      <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">
       legume
      </a>
      plant family support nitrogen-fixing symbionts. Some
      <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">
       cyanobacteria
      </a>
      are also capable of nitrogen fixation. These are
      <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">
       phototrophs
      </a>
      , which carry out photosynthesis. Like other nitrogen-fixing bacteria, they can either be free-living or have symbiotic relationships with plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other sources of nitrogen include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_deposition" title="Acid deposition">
       acid deposition
      </a>
      produced through the combustion of
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuels
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      gas which evaporates from agricultural fields which have had fertilizers applied to them, and dust.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Anthropogenic nitrogen inputs account for about 80% of all nitrogen fluxes in ecosystems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When plant tissues are shed or are eaten, the nitrogen in those tissues becomes available to animals and microbes. Microbial decomposition releases nitrogen compounds from dead organic matter in the soil, where plants, fungi and bacteria compete for it. Some soil bacteria use organic nitrogen-containing compounds as a source of carbon, and release
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">
       ammonium
      </a>
      ions into the soil. This process is known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonification" title="Ammonification">
       nitrogen mineralization
      </a>
      . Others convert ammonium to
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">
       nitrite
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">
       nitrate
      </a>
      ions, a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrification" title="Nitrification">
       nitrification
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">
       Nitric oxide
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxide
      </a>
      are also produced during nitrification.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Under nitrogen-rich and oxygen-poor conditions, nitrates and nitrites are converted to
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       nitrogen gas
      </a>
      , a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">
       denitrification
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin197_16-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin197-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other important nutrients include
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">
       phosphorus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
       sulfur
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
       calcium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">
       potassium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">
       manganese
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin215_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin215-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Phosphorus enters ecosystems through
      <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">
       weathering
      </a>
      . As ecosystems age this supply diminishes, making phosphorus-limitation more common in older landscapes (especially in the tropics).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin215_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin215-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Calcium and sulfur are also produced by weathering, but acid deposition is an important source of sulfur in many ecosystems. Although magnesium and manganese are produced by weathering, exchanges between soil organic matter and living cells account for a significant portion of ecosystem fluxes. Potassium is primarily cycled between living cells and soil organic matter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin215_23-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin215-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Function_and_biodiversity">
       Function and biodiversity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Function and biodiversity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       Biodiversity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_engineer" title="Ecosystem engineer">
       Ecosystem engineer
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_loch_lomond.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/220px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/330px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/440px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_of_loch_lomond.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Loch_Lomond" title="Loch Lomond">
         Loch Lomond
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
         Scotland
        </a>
        forms a relatively isolated ecosystem. The fish community of this lake has remained stable over a long period until a number of
        <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
         introductions
        </a>
        in the 1970s restructured its
        <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">
         food web
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          23
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/220px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/330px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/440px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Spiny forest at Ifaty,
        <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">
         Madagascar
        </a>
        , featuring various
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Adansonia" title="Adansonia">
          Adansonia
         </a>
        </i>
        (baobab) species,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Alluaudia_procera" title="Alluaudia procera">
          Alluaudia procera
         </a>
        </i>
        (Madagascar ocotillo) and other vegetation.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ecosystem processes are broad generalizations that actually take place through the actions of individual organisms. The nature of the organismsthe species,
      <a href="/wiki/Functional_group_(ecology)" title="Functional group (ecology)">
       functional groups
      </a>
      and trophic levels to which they belongdictates the sorts of actions these individuals are capable of carrying out, and the relative efficiency with which they do so. Thus, ecosystem processes are driven by the number of species in an ecosystem, the exact nature of each individual species, and the relative abundance organisms within these species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin265_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin265-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Biodiversity plays an important role in ecosystem functioning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulze449_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schulze449-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Ecological theory suggests that in order to coexist, species must have some level of
      <a href="/wiki/Limiting_similarity" title="Limiting similarity">
       limiting similarity
      </a>
      they must be different from one another in some fundamental way, otherwise one species would
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitive_exclusion" title="Competitive exclusion">
       competitively exclude
      </a>
      the other.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niche_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-niche-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite this, the cumulative effect of additional species in an ecosystem is not linearadditional species may enhance nitrogen retention, for example, but beyond some level of species richness, additional species may have little additive effect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin265_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin265-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The addition (or loss) of species which are ecologically similar to those already present in an ecosystem tends to only have a small effect on ecosystem function. Ecologically distinct species, on the other hand, have a much larger effect. Similarly, dominant species have a large impact on ecosystem function, while rare species tend to have a small effect.
      <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">
       Keystone species
      </a>
      tend to have an effect on ecosystem function that is disproportionate to their abundance in an ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin265_25-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin265-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_goods_and_services">
       Ecosystem goods and services
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ecosystem goods and services">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">
       Ecosystem services
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_goods_and_services" title="Ecological goods and services">
       Ecological goods and services
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_valuation" title="Ecosystem valuation">
       Ecosystem valuation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_yield" title="Ecological yield">
       Ecological yield
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ecosystems provide a variety of goods and services upon which people depend.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ecosystem goods include the "tangible, material products"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2007_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2007-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      of ecosystem processesfood, construction material, medicinal plantsin addition to less tangible items like tourism and recreation, and genes from wild plants and animals that can be used to improve domestic species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ecosystem services, on the other hand, are generally "improvements in the condition or location of things of value".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2007_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2007-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These include things like the maintenance of hydrological cycles, cleaning air and water, the maintenance of oxygen in the atmosphere, crop pollination and even things like beauty, inspiration and opportunities for research.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While ecosystem goods have traditionally been recognized as being the basis for things of economic value, ecosystem services tend to be taken for granted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2007_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2007-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While
      <a href="/wiki/Gretchen_Daily" title="Gretchen Daily">
       Gretchen Daily
      </a>
      's original definition distinguished between ecosystem goods and ecosystem services,
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Costanza" title="Robert Costanza">
       Robert Costanza
      </a>
      and colleagues' later work and that of the
      <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Ecosystem_Assessment" title="Millennium Ecosystem Assessment">
       Millennium Ecosystem Assessment
      </a>
      lumped all of these together as ecosystem services.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2007_29-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2007-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_management">
       Ecosystem management
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ecosystem management">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_management" title="Ecosystem management">
       Ecosystem management
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">
       Ecological economics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">
       Sustainability
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">
       Sustainable development
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      When
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource_management" title="Natural resource management">
       natural resource management
      </a>
      is applied to whole ecosystems, rather than single species, it is termed ecosystem management.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grumbine1994_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grumbine1994-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A variety of definitions exist:
      <a href="/wiki/F._Stuart_Chapin_III" title="F. Stuart Chapin III">
       F. Stuart Chapin
      </a>
      and coauthors define it as "the application of ecological science to resource management to promote long-term sustainability of ecosystems and the delivery of essential ecosystem goods and services",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin362_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin362-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while Norman Christensen and coauthors defined it as "management driven by explicit goals, executed by policies, protocols, and practices, and made adaptable by monitoring and research based on our best understanding of the ecological interactions and processes necessary to sustain ecosystem structure and function"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Peter Brussard and colleagues defined it as "managing areas at various scales in such a way that ecosystem services and biological resources are preserved while appropriate human use and options for livelihood are sustained".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brussard1998_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brussard1998-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although definitions of ecosystem management abound, there is a common set of principles which underlie these definitions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin362_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin362-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A fundamental principle is the long-term sustainability of the production of goods and services by the ecosystem;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin362_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin362-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "intergenerational sustainability [is] a precondition for management, not an afterthought".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It also requires clear goals with respect to future trajectories and behaviors of the system being managed. Other important requirements include a sound ecological understanding of the system, including connectedness, ecological dynamics and the context in which the system is embedded. Other important principles include an understanding of the role of humans as components of the ecosystems and the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_management" title="Adaptive management">
       adaptive management
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While ecosystem management can be used as part of a plan for
      <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">
       wilderness
      </a>
      conservation, it can also be used in intensively managed ecosystems
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen1998_28-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christensen1998-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (see, for example,
      <a href="/wiki/Agroecosystem" title="Agroecosystem">
       agroecosystem
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Close_to_nature_forestry" title="Close to nature forestry">
       close to nature forestry
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_dynamics">
       Ecosystem dynamics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecosystem dynamics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg/220px-HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg/330px-HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg/440px-HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HohRiverTrail_7322.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Temperate_rainforest" title="Temperate rainforest">
         Temperate rainforest
        </a>
        on the
        <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Peninsula" title="Olympic Peninsula">
         Olympic Peninsula
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">
         Washington state
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avalanche_Lake,_looking_south.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg/220px-Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg/330px-Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg/440px-Avalanche_Lake%2C_looking_south.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avalanche_Lake,_looking_south.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/High_Peaks_Wilderness_Area" title="High Peaks Wilderness Area">
         High Peaks Wilderness Area
        </a>
        in the 6,000,000-acre (2,400,000ha)
        <a href="/wiki/Adirondack_Park" title="Adirondack Park">
         Adirondack Park
        </a>
        is an example of a diverse ecosystem.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ecosystems are dynamic entitiesinvariably, they are subject to periodic disturbances and are in the process of recovering from some past disturbance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When an ecosystem is subject to some sort of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perturbation_(biology)" title="Perturbation (biology)">
       perturbation
      </a>
      , it responds by moving away from its initial state. The tendency of a system to remain close to its equilibrium state, despite that disturbance, is termed its
      <a href="/wiki/Resistance_(ecology)" title="Resistance (ecology)">
       resistance
      </a>
      . On the other hand, the speed with which it returns to its initial state after disturbance is called its
      <a href="/wiki/Resilience_(ecology)" title="Resilience (ecology)">
       resilience
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      From one year to another, ecosystems experience variation in their biotic and abiotic environments. A drought, an especially cold winter and a pest outbreak all constitute short-term variability in environmental conditions. Animal populations vary from year to year, building up during resource-rich periods and crashing as they overshoot their food supply. These changes play out in changes in NPP, decomposition rates, and other ecosystem processes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Longer-term changes also shape ecosystem processesthe forests of eastern North America still show legacies of cultivation which ceased 200 years ago, while methane production in eastern Siberian lakes is controlled by organic matter which accumulated during the
      <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
       Pleistocene
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Disturbance_(ecology)" title="Disturbance (ecology)">
       Disturbance
      </a>
      also plays an important role in ecological processes.
      <a href="/wiki/F._Stuart_Chapin_III" title="F. Stuart Chapin III">
       F. Stuart Chapin
      </a>
      and coauthors define disturbance as "a relatively discrete event in time and space that alters the structure of populations, communities and ecosystems and causes changes in resources availability or the physical environment".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin_285_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin_285-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This can range from tree falls and insect outbreaks to hurricanes and wildfires to volcanic eruptions and can cause large changes in plant, animal and microbe populations, as well soil organic matter content.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Disturbance is followed by
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">
       succession
      </a>
      , a "directional change in ecosystem structure and functioning resulting from biotically driven changes in resources supply."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin_285_33-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin_285-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
