      The frequency and severity of disturbance determines the way it impacts ecosystem function. Major disturbance like a volcanic eruption or glacial advance and retreat leave behind soils that lack plants, animals or organic matter. Ecosystems that experience disturbances that undergo
      <a href="/wiki/Primary_succession" title="Primary succession">
       primary succession
      </a>
      . Less severe disturbance like forest fires, hurricanes or cultivation result in
      <a href="/wiki/Secondary_succession" title="Secondary succession">
       secondary succession
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More severe disturbance and more frequent disturbance result in longer recovery times. Ecosystems recover more quickly from less severe disturbance events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The early stages of primary succession are dominated by species with small propagules (seed and spores) which can be dispersed long distances. The early colonizersoften
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alga" title="Alga">
       algae
      </a>
      , cyanobacteria and
      <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">
       lichens
      </a>
      stabilize the substrate. Nitrogen supplies are limited in new soils, and nitrogen-fixing species tend to play an important role early in primary succession. Unlike in primary succession, the species that dominate secondary succession, are usually present from the start of the process, often in the
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_seed_bank" title="Soil seed bank">
       soil seed bank
      </a>
      . In some systems the successional pathways are fairly consistent, and thus, are easy to predict. In others, there are many possible pathwaysfor example, the introduced nitrogen-fixing legume,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Myrica_faya" title="Myrica faya">
        Myrica faya
       </a>
      </i>
      , alter successional trajectories in Hawaiian forests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapin281_9-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chapin281-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_ecology" title="Theoretical ecology">
       theoretical ecologist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ulanowicz" title="Robert Ulanowicz">
       Robert Ulanowicz
      </a>
      has used
      <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">
       information theory
      </a>
      tools to describe the structure of ecosystems, emphasizing mutual information (correlations) in studied systems. Drawing on this methodology and prior observations of complex ecosystems, Ulanowicz depicts approaches to determining the stress levels on ecosystems and predicting system reactions to defined types of alteration in their settings (such as increased or reduced energy flow, and
      <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">
       eutrophication
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_ecology">
       Ecosystem ecology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ecosystem ecology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_ecology" title="Ecosystem ecology">
       Ecosystem ecology
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_model" title="Ecosystem model">
       Ecosystem model
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1447" data-file-width="2031" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif/lossy-page1-220px-East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif/lossy-page1-330px-East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif/lossy-page1-440px-East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East_Scotia_Ridge_-_Plos_Biol_04.tif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">
         hydrothermal vent
        </a>
        is an ecosystem on the ocean floor. (The scale bar is 1 m.)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ecosystem ecology studies "the flow of energy and materials through organisms and the physical environment". It seeks to understand the processes which govern the stocks of material and energy in ecosystems, and the flow of matter and energy through them. The study of ecosystems can cover 10
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
       orders of magnitude
      </a>
      , from the surface layers of rocks to the surface of the planet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is no single definition of what constitutes an ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagoff2003_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sagoff2003-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      German ecologist
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ernst-Detlef_Schulze&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ernst-Detlef Schulze (page does not exist)">
       Ernst-Detlef Schulze
      </a>
      and coauthors defined an ecosystem as an area which is "uniform regarding the biological turnover, and contains all the fluxes above and below the ground area under consideration." They explicitly reject
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_Likens" title="Gene Likens">
       Gene Likens
      </a>
      ' use of entire river catchments as "too wide a demarcation" to be a single ecosystem, given the level of heterogeneity within such an area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulze399_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schulze399-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other authors have suggested that an ecosystem can encompass a much larger area, even the whole planet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planet_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-planet-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Schulze and coauthors also rejected the idea that a single rotting log could be studied as an ecosystem because the size of the flows between the log and its surroundings are too large, relative to the proportion cycles within the log.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulze399_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schulze399-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Philosopher of science
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mark_Sagoff&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mark Sagoff (page does not exist)">
       Mark Sagoff
      </a>
      considers the failure to define "the kind of object it studies" to be an obstacle to the development of theory in ecosystem ecology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagoff2003_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sagoff2003-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Ecosystems can be studied through a variety of approachestheoretical studies, studies monitoring specific ecosystems over long periods of time, those that look at differences between ecosystems to elucidate how they work and direct manipulative experimentation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carpenter1998_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1998-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies can be carried out at a variety of scales, from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microcosm:_Model_/_experimental_ecosystem" title="Microcosm: Model / experimental ecosystem">
       microcosms
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mesocosm" title="Mesocosm">
       mesocosms
      </a>
      which serve as simplified representations of ecosystems, through whole-ecosystem studies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schindler1998_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schindler1998-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      American ecologist
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_R._Carpenter" title="Stephen R. Carpenter">
       Stephen R. Carpenter
      </a>
      has argued that microcosm experiments can be "irrelevant and diversionary" if they are not carried out in conjunction with field studies carried out at the ecosystem scale, because microcosm experiments often fail to accurately predict ecosystem-level dynamics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carpenter1996_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1996-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubbard_Brook_Ecosystem_Study" title="Hubbard Brook Ecosystem Study">
       Hubbard Brook Ecosystem Study
      </a>
      , established in the
      <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">
       White Mountains, New Hampshire
      </a>
      in 1963, was the first successful attempt to study an entire watershed as an ecosystem. The study used stream chemistry as a means of monitoring ecosystem properties, and developed a detailed
      <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemistry" title="Biogeochemistry">
       biogeochemical model
      </a>
      of the ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindenmayer2010_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lindenmayer2010-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Long_Term_Ecological_Research_Network" title="Long Term Ecological Research Network">
       Long-term research
      </a>
      at the site led to the discovery of
      <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">
       acid rain
      </a>
      in North America in 1972, and was able to document the consequent depletion of soil
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">
       cations
      </a>
      (especially calcium) over the next several decades.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Likens2004_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Likens2004-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Classification">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_diversity" title="Ecosystem diversity">
       Ecosystem diversity
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">
       Ecoregion
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_land_classification" title="Ecological land classification">
       Ecological land classification
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecotope" title="Ecotope">
       Ecotope
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baja_California_Desert.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2100" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Baja_California_Desert.jpg/220px-Baja_California_Desert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Baja_California_Desert.jpg/330px-Baja_California_Desert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Baja_California_Desert.jpg/440px-Baja_California_Desert.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baja_California_Desert.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">
         Flora
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Desert" title="Baja California Desert">
         Baja California Desert
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Catavi%C3%B1a" title="Catavia">
         Catavia
        </a>
        region,
        <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
         Mexico
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Classifying ecosystems into ecologically homogeneous units is an important step towards effective ecosystem management.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lugo1999_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lugo1999-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A variety of systems exist, based on vegetation cover, remote sensing, and
      <a href="/wiki/Holdridge_life_zones" title="Holdridge life zones">
       bioclimatic classification
      </a>
      systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lugo1999_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lugo1999-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      American geographer
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bailey_(geographer)" title="Robert Bailey (geographer)">
       Robert Bailey
      </a>
      defines a hierarchy of ecosystem units ranging from microecosystems (individual homogeneous sites, on the order of 10 square kilometres (4sqmi) in area), through mesoecosystems (
      <a href="/wiki/Landscape_ecology" title="Landscape ecology">
       landscape mosaics
      </a>
      , on the order of 1,000 square kilometres (400sqmi)) to macroecosystems (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecoregions" title="Ecoregions">
       ecoregions
      </a>
      , on the order of 100,000 square kilometres (40,000sqmi)).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaileyCh2_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BaileyCh2-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Bailey outlined five different methods for identifying ecosystems:
      <i>
       <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gestalt" title="wikt:gestalt">
        gestalt
       </a>
      </i>
      ("a whole that is not derived through considerable of its parts"), in which regions are recognized and boundaries drawn intuitively; a map overlay system where different layers like
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">
       landforms
      </a>
      and soil types are overlain to identify ecosystems;
      <a href="/wiki/Cluster_analysis" title="Cluster analysis">
       multivariate clustering
      </a>
      of site attributes;
      <a href="/wiki/Digital_image_processing" title="Digital image processing">
       digital image processing
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">
       remotely sensed
      </a>
      data grouping areas based on their appearance or other
      <a href="/wiki/Multispectral_image" title="Multispectral image">
       spectral
      </a>
      properties; or by a "controlling factors method" where a subset of factors (like soils, climate, vegetation
      <a href="/wiki/Physiognomy" title="Physiognomy">
       physiognomy
      </a>
      or the
      <a href="/wiki/Species_distribution" title="Species distribution">
       distribution
      </a>
      of plant or animal species) are selected from a large array of possible ones are used to delineate ecosystems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaileyCh3_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BaileyCh3-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast with Bailey's methodology, Puerto Rico ecologist
      <a href="/wiki/Ariel_Lugo" title="Ariel Lugo">
       Ariel Lugo
      </a>
      and coauthors identified ten characteristics of an effective classification system: that it be based on
      <a href="/wiki/Georeference" title="Georeference">
       georeferenced
      </a>
      , quantitative data; that it should minimize subjectivity and explicitly identify criteria and assumptions; that it should be structured around the factors that drive ecosystem processes; that it should reflect the hierarchical nature of ecosystems; that it should be flexible enough to conform to the various scales at which ecosystem management operates; that it should be tied to reliable measures of climate so that it can "anticipat[e] global climate change; that it be applicable worldwide; that it should be validated against independent data; that it take into account the sometimes complex relationship between climate, vegetation and ecosystem functioning; and that it should be able to adapt and improve as new data become available".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lugo1999_43-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lugo1999-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Types">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">

       </a>
       <ul>

     </ul>
     <div class="center">
      <div class="thumb tnone">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panorama_presa_las_ni%C3%B1as_mogan_gran_canaria.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="175" data-file-width="800" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/Panorama_presa_las_ni%C3%B1as_mogan_gran_canaria.jpg" width="800"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">

         <center>
          A
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">

          </a>

          <a href="/wiki/Gran_Canaria" title="Gran Canaria">
           Gran Canaria
          </a>

          <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">

     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_threats">
       Anthropogenic threats
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecosystem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic threats">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundaries" title="Planetary boundaries">
       Planetary boundaries
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As human populations grow, so do the resource demands imposed on ecosystems and the impacts of the human
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">
       ecological footprint
      </a>
      . Natural resources are not invulnerable and infinitely available. The environmental impacts of
      <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">
       anthropogenic
      </a>
      actions, which are processes or materials derived from human activities, are becoming more apparentair and water quality are increasingly compromised, oceans are being
      <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">
       overfished
      </a>
      , pests and diseases are extending beyond their historical boundaries, and
      <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
       deforestation
      </a>
      is exacerbating flooding downstream. It has been reported that approximately 4050% of Earth's ice-free land surface has been heavily transformed or degraded by anthropogenic activities, 66% of marine
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">
       fisheries
      </a>
      are either
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overexploited" title="Overexploited">
       overexploited
      </a>
      or at their limit,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_CO2" title="Atmospheric CO2">
       atmospheric CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </a>
      has increased more than 30% since the advent of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">
       industrialization
      </a>
      , and nearly 25% of Earth's bird species have gone
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">
       extinct
      </a>
      in the last two thousand years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Society is increasingly becoming aware that
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">
       ecosystem services
      </a>
      are not only limited, but also that they are threatened by human activities. The need to better consider long-term
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_health" title="Ecosystem health">
       ecosystem health
      </a>
      and its role in enabling human habitation and economic activity is urgent. To help inform decision-makers, many ecosystem services are being assigned economic values, often based on the cost of replacement with anthropogenic alternatives. The ongoing challenge of prescribing economic value to nature, for example through
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_banking" title="Biodiversity banking">
       biodiversity banking
      </a>
      , is prompting transdisciplinary shifts in how we recognize and manage the environment,
      <a href="/wiki/Social_responsibility" title="Social responsibility">
       social responsibility
      </a>
      , business opportunities, and our future as a species.
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-1">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-1">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Hatcher, Bruce Gordon (1990). "Coral reef primary productivity. A hierarchy of pattern and process".
          <i>
           Trends in Ecology and Evolution
          </i>
          <b>
           5
          </b>
          (5): 149155.
          <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
           doi
